<div id="review_2416503790" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2416503790" />
    <a title="Mercedes Rochelle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4548783-mercedes-rochelle"><img alt="Mercedes Rochelle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442490757p2/4548783.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2416503790?book_show_action=true">Jun 30, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mercedes Rochelle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mercedes Rochelle" href="/user/show/4548783-mercedes-rochelle">Mercedes Rochelle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2416503790" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13744248044554207254">Even though I read my classics back in my college days, they stuck in my mind for forty years. I always wondered how the Trojans treated Helen, who they knew was the source of all their troubles. Happily, Margaret George gives us a sympathetic and thoroughly believable account from Helen’s point of view. As daughter to Leda and Zeus (the swan), she had an otherworldly glow that immediately singled her out as someone special, which goes a long way toward explaining why she was so pivotal in the w</span>
  <span id="freeText13744248044554207254" style="display:none">Even though I read my classics back in my college days, they stuck in my mind for forty years. I always wondered how the Trojans treated Helen, who they knew was the source of all their troubles. Happily, Margaret George gives us a sympathetic and thoroughly believable account from Helen’s point of view. As daughter to Leda and Zeus (the swan), she had an otherworldly glow that immediately singled her out as someone special, which goes a long way toward explaining why she was so pivotal in the war. And yet our Helen in this book was never vain about her beauty; in fact, she saw it as a curse (usually). She really only wanted to be happy with her true lover Paris and saw her role in the Greek invasion as more of an excuse for the warlike Agamemnon to invade and plunder. Menelaus was more of a pitiful figure; although Helen chose him as her husband, she was soon disillusioned and repelled by him. When Paris came along, naturally there was no contest. Of course, the Trojans were not happy when he brought Helen home, but they were tolerant of her; after all, their course was already determined by the Gods. When the Greeks came, many of the Trojans were excited about fighting and for a short time Helen was exalted—that is, until the real killing started. From there on, the reader must suffer along with the women left behind in the city, for we know what is coming. This is a beautiful tale of love, guilt, and sadness, and I really think it does credit to the original stories. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13744248044554207254" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_232750531" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/232750531" />
    <a title="scissorstoariadne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6763921-scissorstoariadne"><img alt="scissorstoariadne" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/232750531?book_show_action=true">Nov 07, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="scissorstoariadne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="scissorstoariadne" href="/user/show/6763921-scissorstoariadne">scissorstoariadne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer232750531" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8996872407845194476">Greek mythology, or mythology, in general, is and will always be one of my great loves. (I say this at the risk of sounding completely demented) The story of Helen and the fall of Troy was never my most favourite myth but recently, I started to gravitate towards it and am very fascinated with many of the secondary characters involved. I was pleased to read that George had included them with sufficient backstory: Cassandra, Clytemnestra, Aphrodite, Athena, Electra, and Orestes. <br/><br/>What was really en</span>
  <span id="freeText8996872407845194476" style="display:none">Greek mythology, or mythology, in general, is and will always be one of my great loves. (I say this at the risk of sounding completely demented) The story of Helen and the fall of Troy was never my most favourite myth but recently, I started to gravitate towards it and am very fascinated with many of the secondary characters involved. I was pleased to read that George had included them with sufficient backstory: Cassandra, Clytemnestra, Aphrodite, Athena, Electra, and Orestes. <br /><br />What was really entertaining about this novel is that George really captured the beguiling charm of a good ol' Greek myth in its setting. The inclusion and interactions of the characters were believable, yet equally magical. <br /><br />What irked me is that Helen of Troy is overly long, making it a bit of a drag when the tension was shifting between events. George is occasionally abrupt and sparse with language, which might have compounded this. Plus, Helen, at times, felt a little too passive and introspective to my liking, since I expected her to be one of the more vivacious women of Greek mythology. But I did like her maternal instinct throughout the book. Switching between narrators might have made the novel a bit more invigorating; I would have loved to hear from, perhaps, Clytemnestra, or Achilles. <br /><br />Overall, it was an enriching read, albeit a little slow-moving. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8996872407845194476" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4479407550031557800">Okay, Margaret George is an extremely "wordy" author. She likes adjectives and she likes to use them a lot. I am a simple woman, if the day is hot then I don't want to hear about how the sweat drops slide down your face.<br/>Also, I think she got bored in the last 100 pages because Helen just kind of summarizes things and then the book is over.<br/>Also, I hate Helen of Troy.   <span id="freeText4479407550031557800" style="display:none">Okay, Margaret George is an extremely "wordy" author. She likes adjectives and she likes to use them a lot. I am a simple woman, if the day is hot then I don't want to hear about how the sweat drops slide down your face.<br />Also, I think she got bored in the last 100 pages because Helen just kind of summarizes things and then the book is over.<br />Also, I hate Helen of Troy. If I was to write a fiction book about a fiction woman I would make Helen of Troy a power house. I don't CARE that Homer didn't write her like that. <b>I</b> would write her like that. To me, Helen would be in the war room planning the attacks. She would be on the field with the Amazon Women thrusting and cutting her way to freedom.<br />Instead, George turned her into a whiney, childish, frightened woman and the only reason I finished this book was because it was hot in my apartment, I didn't want to move and I have weekends off.<br /><br />Seriously.</span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3887720-christin?shelf=greeks-and-romans-too">greeks-and-romans-too</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3887720-christin?shelf=classical-historical-fiction">classical-historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3887720-christin?shelf=odysseus-and-friends">odysseus-and-friends</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer169693264" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7408106405794860641">It's a perfectly adequate version of the story where Paris is noble, Menelaus is a chump, Agamemnon is a dick, and Helen just can't help herself. Which is a fine way of going about it. <br/><br/>But one of the reviews on the back said it can be shelved next to Mary Renault and uh-uh honey no. The difference is, Mary will make you <i>believe.</i> If I picked up another book about Helen where she was a conniving beeyotch or was actually abducted by Paris, it wouldn't be a problem, I could see that. But after readi</span>
  <span id="freeText7408106405794860641" style="display:none">It's a perfectly adequate version of the story where Paris is noble, Menelaus is a chump, Agamemnon is a dick, and Helen just can't help herself. Which is a fine way of going about it. <br /><br />But one of the reviews on the back said it can be shelved next to Mary Renault and uh-uh honey no. The difference is, Mary will make you <i>believe.</i> If I picked up another book about Helen where she was a conniving beeyotch or was actually abducted by Paris, it wouldn't be a problem, I could see that. But after reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/93941.The_King_Must_Die" title="The King Must Die by Mary Renault" rel="nofollow noopener">The King Must Die</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/248632.The_Bull_from_the_Sea" title="The Bull from the Sea by Mary Renault" rel="nofollow noopener">Bull From the Sea</a> there's just no other way for Theseus to be. That IS Theseus and damn all the haters. (Are there any haters?) </span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_317948975" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/317948975" />
    <a title="Rick Davis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2382227-rick-davis"><img alt="Rick Davis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528314840p2/2382227.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/317948975?book_show_action=true">Apr 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rick Davis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rick Davis" href="/user/show/2382227-rick-davis">Rick Davis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2382227-rick-davis?shelf=historical-fiction-ancient">historical-fiction-ancient</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer317948975" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17562988847622005107"><i>Helen of Troy</i> by Margaret George is an ambitious book. The author attempts to create an autobiography for the mythical Helen encompassing her entire life from childhood through “death”. At six hundred thirty-eight pages, it stands at about the same length as Homer’s epic tale of the Trojan War. However, upon finishing Margaret George’s book, my lament was “Ichabod”, the glory has departed.<br/><br/><b>Positives</b><br/><br/>Before I explain what I mean by that and why I think that this book, ambitious as it is, fails to a</span>
  <span id="freeText17562988847622005107" style="display:none"><i>Helen of Troy</i> by Margaret George is an ambitious book. The author attempts to create an autobiography for the mythical Helen encompassing her entire life from childhood through “death”. At six hundred thirty-eight pages, it stands at about the same length as Homer’s epic tale of the Trojan War. However, upon finishing Margaret George’s book, my lament was “Ichabod”, the glory has departed.<br /><br /><b>Positives</b><br /><br />Before I explain what I mean by that and why I think that this book, ambitious as it is, fails to achieve its purpose, I would like to share the things I thought the book got right. First of all, George has done her research well. The customs, architecture, props, and landscape of Sparta in the 13th century BC are wonderfully recreated according to the best knowledge we can have of the time. She attempts to be realistic and historical in her portrayal even of stories that are, at best, legendary. In this sense the book reminded me of <i>The King Must Die</i> by Mary Renault, which does the same thing, albeit more successfully, with the story of Theseus and the Minotaur. The second thing that George gets right is her inclusion of the gods in her telling of the story. Any reading of the legendary accounts of the Trojan War leaves the sense that there is no possibility that these events could have transpired with the gods pulling some strings behind the scenes. This is why the movie “Troy” was a failure and one of the reasons why Roger Ebert quipped that Homer’s estate should sue the filmmakers. However despite these positive aspects of the book, there are several weaknesses that are hard to overlook.<br /><br /><b>The Character of Helen</b><br /><br />The first weakness of the book is in the characterization of Helen. Since the book is an autobiography, we know from the outset that Helen is going to be presented as a sympathetic character. I even went into the book willing to go along with Helen and sympathize with her. However, in this important point George does not succeed. To show why this is the case, it’s important to look at the traditional depictions of Helen, both positive and negative, and to compare them to the portrayal in this book.<br /><br />Helen is a woman who left her husband and ran away with a man more than ten years her junior because he was more handsome than her husband. This might not have been a big deal for an ordinary person, though it would have still been immoral. But Helen is the queen of Sparta, and therefore her perceived abduction by a Trojan ambassador and prince is seen as an act of aggression against Sparta and a cause for war. How can a woman performing an act so clearly immoral and irresponsible be viewed in any sort of positive light? Homer’s answer appears to be twofold. In <i>The Iliad</i> and <i>The Odyssey</i>, we get the sense that Helen was deceived by Aphrodite in some sort of mind-control/hypnotic sort of way to go with Paris back to Troy. Aphrodite did this as a reward for Paris because he chose her as the winner of a beauty contest over Hera and Athena. In <i>The Iliad</i>, we also see that Helen despises Paris and is essentially a prisoner in Troy, bullied by Aphrodite and kept by Paris’s lust. This is a more positive depiction of Helen. For those who find this implausible, get rid of Aphrodite and cast the story in a modern world. How many women leave their husbands for someone they see as a “better man” only to find out later that he is not all they thought he was and are stuck with him because they are either afraid of him or don’t know where else to go? This is a common enough story and conjures up sympathy for the woman trapped in such a sorry situation. Even though she has done wrong, she is caught in her own snare and is to be pitied. However, though George includes the scene from <i>The Iliad</i> where Helen despises Paris, it turns out to be a simple misunderstanding, easily remedied. The Helen of this book is influenced by but not essentially deceived by Aphrodite. All Aphrodite does is to make Helen not enjoy making love with her husband Menelaus and enjoy making love with Paris. Because we know that the value of a marriage is tied up only with the amount of sexual enjoyment we get from it, right? Helen has few misgivings in this book and believes to the end that she has done nothing essentially wrong.<br /><br />This seems to leave no other option than the common portrayal of Helen by the playwrights of the golden age of Athens. The Athenian playwrights represent Helen as a capricious whore who leaves her husband for another man for no good reason other than the fact that she feels like it. Sorry; lovey-dovey feelings do not nullify wedding vows. How does George get around this inevitable conclusion? She uses a tactic that is less than convincing. After pining after Paris, meeting with him a nighttime, and fantasizing about running away with him, she walks in on Menelaus having an affair with one of the servants. Thus she is clearly justified in running of with Paris, right? Here’s the problem with this scenario; she has clearly been toying with adulterous thoughts for Paris before she ever had any hint of Menelaus’s infidelity. The addition of a second wrong does not make the first wrong right. The revelation of Menelaus’s affair does not cause us sympathize with Helen so much as it causes us to simply despise Menelaus as well. So much for the character of Helen. When the Greek commanders later hurl invective at her and call her a whore, the reader is supposed to feel that these insults are unjust. However, given what see of Helen’s character, it’s hard to disagree with the Greeks here. George would have been much better off going with Homer’s version of the story and creating a conflicted, abused and tragic heroine rather than someone who revels in her adultery.<br /><br /><b>The Character of the Greeks</b><br /><br />One of the most notable things about <i>The Iliad</i>, and one of the tragedies of war in real life, is that there are noble and good people on both sides. Hector, the hero of Troy, is a great and brave man, but so are the Greek leaders Achilles, Menelaus, Agamemnon, the Aiantes, Diomedes, and others. In George’s book, the Greeks are almost to a man despicable, petty, cruel and lecherous. The level of her negative portrayal of the Greeks is so cartoonish that one is tempted to picture the entire Achaean force wearing black capes and top hats with appropriately long mustaches to twirl.<br /><br />I think the reason that George falls into this trap is that, while getting all the physical details of the culture right, the customs and the costumes so to speak, the character of Helen in this book thinks predominately like a modern. The book does not enter fully into an ancient mindset, and so the characters are all being judged by a modern worldview that really does have a hard time understanding the glory of the soldiers in the Trojan War. Achilles gets the worst of this treatment. In Homer, Achilles is a noble warrior, denied his “arête” by Agamemnon, caught in a choice between a short life with glory or a long life in obscurity, haunted by the realization that he should have been a god as powerful as Zeus had it not been for Zeus’s meddling interference. Where is this noble and conflicted character in <i>Helen of Troy</i>? He is gone, replaced by a whining, screeching, spoiled brat with the mental capacity of a Neanderthal. Because of this same tendency to refuse to portray the nobility and glory of the Greeks, the essential tragedy and human interest that characterizes <i>The Iliad</i> vanishes. The only human emotions allowed to be portrayed in this book are emotions that fit comfortably within the realm of modern paperback romance. Anything that conflicts with these norms, including true manliness to some extent, is seen as inhuman and ugly.<br /><br />There is a scene in <i>The Iliad</i> that I consider to be the most moving scene in the book, and possibly the most moving scene in all of human literature. It is the scene where the old king Priam goes to Achilles to ask for the body of his son Hector whom Achilles has slain in battle, or “murdered” if you prefer George’s wording. While Priam is supplicating Achilles, a moment of understanding passes between them during which Achilles sees in Priam his own aged father whom he will never see again, and Priam sees in Achilles the glory of his own dead son Hector and is comforted in the fact that Hector has been killed by such a noble soldier. They weep openly together, and the reader, if he or she has a heart, weeps with them, two ordinary and good people caught up in something bigger than themselves and unable to extricate themselves from the situation. It is not surprising that this scene is completely downplayed in George’s book. She can’t risk a sympathetic portrayal of Achilles because it would destroy her cartoonish sense that Achilles is simply “the bad guy” and nothing more. Priam goes to beg for Hector’s body, but it is seen as shameful and we get no hint that anything remotely human ever passes between Priam and Achilles.<br /><br /><b>Summary</b><br /><br />There is more that I could say about this book, but after all this is a blog and if you’re still reading this you’ve gone much further than most internet readers would. So, I’ll summarize. This book unfortunately fails at what it sets out to do. The portrayal of the events is too simplistic to feel real in the same way that Homer’s <i>Iliad</i> feels real. The “follow your heart” and “love conquers all” theme is worthy of a modern romance, but not an ancient story. The characters do not seem to fit into their carefully crafted historical surroundings because all of the “bad” characters are seen through a modern lens as brutal barbarians and all the “good” characters share more than a few modern attitudes themselves. Ultimately this book is not satisfying because the author does not fully enter into the worldview of the time period about which she is writing. I would not recommend this book as an introduction to the story of Helen of Troy, but would rather encourage interested readers to plunge directly into Homer’s writings or the plays of writers like Euripides and Aeschylus. Don’t be intimidated by the classics. They are most often easier to read than you’ve been led to believe, and they are classics for a reason. Unlike this book, they touch upon something essential and indelible in the human soul.<br /><br />1.5/5 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="17562988847622005107" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_427635697" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/427635697" />
    <a title="Patrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1383849-patrick"><img alt="Patrick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1226811925p2/1383849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/427635697?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick" href="/user/show/1383849-patrick">Patrick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330418911" href="/book/show/882627.Helen_of_Troy">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer427635697&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer427635697" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5541180031230503245">I give this book 5 stars because I love mythological epics.<br/><br/>I think that legends can be created by twisting the facts of what truly happened. And people who are different can be given narrative that is not based on fact but conflated to serve some sort of purpose.<br/><br/>Helen is a rambunctious child and just as all people especially children the more forbidden the more she pursues it such as seeing her face and going out into the world. It sucks being a beautiful aristocratic woman because your worth is</span>
  <span id="freeText5541180031230503245" style="display:none">I give this book 5 stars because I love mythological epics.<br /><br />I think that legends can be created by twisting the facts of what truly happened. And people who are different can be given narrative that is not based on fact but conflated to serve some sort of purpose.<br /><br />Helen is a rambunctious child and just as all people especially children the more forbidden the more she pursues it such as seeing her face and going out into the world. It sucks being a beautiful aristocratic woman because your worth is tied to ones beauty. Helen's face was said to be so beautiful that she was descended by Zeus. The King knowing the value of beauty to men was said to place a veil on Helen so it would engage men's imagination. Leda is correct in stating what separates children from adults is the former's innocent imagination. Hermonie is a tomboy who chooses to stay in Sparta rather than follow her mother to Troy.<br /><br />Even in ancient Greece, there was a check to the Kings/Queens vanity through the religious right of insults. <br /><br />George's Menalaus is a good guy who understands Helen's need for quiet amid the storm of gossip. Helen is excited by Odysseus plan for her to choose her suitor instead of her father. When Helen is of marrying age, she wisely realizes that she is choosing more than a man; she is choosing a way of life. She wants to remain in Sparta thus she did not want to marry a king. Although uncertainty drove Helen to a decision she was not ready to make, she wanted freedom to experience and explore life not to marry. To both Menelaus and Helen, marriage to each other meant freedom to both. <br /><br />Menelaus is content and does not care for glory or things but he needs a muse in the form of Helen to unleash his passion and get him to do the extraordinary. In their marriage ceremony, it was a bad omen that a gold chain that that Menelaus gave her felt like a heavy yoke of being tied down. Although he felt fortunate to be married to Helen, his desire for her blinded him to her readiness for him. While Helen realizes that Menelaus is faithful companion to her, she is not physically attracted to him. He is a good husband but not a lover and confidant that she expected. He is not happy because he realizes that Helen is not physically attracted to him. When he became king, he became too busy to be troubled by marital issues much to Helen's relief.<br /><br />By setting a trap to avert a prophesy, Odysseus instead fulfilled it by having all Helen's suitors swear to the gods to defend the marriage of Helen and her choice with their swords and their lives. She pegs Odysseus as a calculating opportunist.<br /><br />Clytemnestra wanted the challenge of a curse that plagued Agamemnon's family to overcome and beat. They are equally fiery for each other and thus are equally matched. Agamemnon is excited by the glory of battle and all that means including loot. He is itching to do battle with Troy but needs an excuse to do it while Menelaus only wants to rule his kingdom justly. Unwittingly, Helen later gives him the excuse to do it. I wonder if George is critiquing W's neoconservative looking for an excuse to attack Iraq. I think it is interesting how George comments how the advance of military technology demands its use in matters of war. <br /><br />Patroclus has a calming effect on Achilles. Is the wise an inquisitive Gelanor Zeus? If not, then why is he so tied to Helen?<br /><br />Helen is correct in stating the most dangerous enemies are those disguised as friends. It is true that there are people out there who hate others simply because the gods blessed them, for Helen it is due to her looks. In the temple of Asclepius, the serpents gave Helen the gift of prescience. <br /><br />Helen is bored and is yearning for adventure which Paris could provide as a vagabond youth who later becomes a prince. It was curiosity of Gelenor's mind that led her to the arms of Aphrodite. In a cave, Aphrodite visited Helen and gave her the gift of desire for life masked as grace. Just like all people who have experienced grace, Helen's senses were sharpened after the encounter. Too bad Aphrodite made a deal with Paris to give Helen to him not her husband. Despite the prophesies of the destruction of Troy by Paris hand, a lot of Greeks will parish via Helen's hands and leaving her beloved Sparta, parents, and daughter, her all consuming desire of Paris was Aphrodite's gift to Helen which was lacking in her marriage with Menelaus. Paris is Helen's muse. Just like Leda, Helen will be struck with desire for another that is not her husband.<br /><br />In George's story, Menelaus cheats on Helen with a beautiful slave to give parity to the cheating of the heart that has taken hold on Helen which frees her from guilt and paves the way for her to go with Paris. I like how George asks which is the greater sin Menelaus physical infidelity due to his lust or Helen's emotional infidelity that will later be consummated? What is the point of marriage without physical attraction? I like how George uses the imagery of the snake to double as both Christian and Greek mythology as a tool for temptation or fate, respectively. <br /><br />I like the description that George gives of a feeling of timelessness spent in a lovers arms. I love how she describes what it is truly like to want a sacred union to the one you love. Helen and Paris consecrated their love by making love on Aphrodite's shine that they made to her at the place of her birth. I like how George makes a philosophical distinction between Helen causing the war and faulting her for it. Can one truly find fault if fate decrees it to happen? Perhaps it was her age of 25 that has made her ready for love instead of her initially being 16. By going with Paris, she became truly free from protocol and being a Queen and thus happy. Although younger, Paris had more experience in life than the sheltered Helen. Aided by Paris' attitude of carpe diem, Helen strove to think of her life in the here and now instead of its future impossibility. In the fields of Troy, Helen divorced Menelaus and tied herself for eternity to Paris. The solemnity of their vows was greater than her wedding ceremony to Menelaus. <br /><br />Danger already accompanied Helen and Paris voyage by having pirates go after them. For the first time, Helen meets the flip side of desire, war and death. It is therefore fitting that Ares was present during their consecration rights to Aphrodite. Troy's soldiers were beginning to resent the lovers because while they were frolicking at sea, the soldiers were burying their dead.<br /><br />TROY:<br /><br />Gelanor is wise in saying the wealth and power are what underpins beauty. Troy's power lies in the fact that it is an important trade city and it is isolated from warring states. I think this is the secret to American success and Washington is correct in stating that we should avoid European wars and to focus on growing the American economy for it is always prolonged wars that destroys nations either externally or internally by depleting the treasury. Like America, Troy was built because of its trade and the diversity that that trade brings in. Helen is attracted to this diversity that Troy brings in. Not only Troy but Priam and his family have been blessed with health, wealth, familial love, and life-long friends/councilors. <br /><br />At least, the Trojans had some time to prepare for war. It seems like all bored young men, the Trojans were as eager for war as the Greeks. Deiphobus and Antimochus were Trojan equivalent of Agamemnon. Some men are really geared towards war. When Menelaus and Odysseus demanded to see Helen, they were both kept away. While Paris thinks it is the warmongering Greeks who kept them from seeing Menelaus/Odysseus, Helen equally thinks it could have been a Trojan who wanted war who did it. Since Agamemnon knows he cannot win against the Trojan walls, the Greeks knowing that they have the numbers thus discouraging the Trojans from making a frontal assault are waging a war of attrition slowly constricting Trojan trade and supplies. When winter arrived, the Greeks also decided to attack Troy's allies and thereby gain supplies for their army. The problem with the Trojan confederation of separate states is that they are not united when the Greeks attack thus they were picked off one by one. Because, Troy did not call on the confederation for soldiers for fear of draining their own supplies, they must now feed the refugee's from the confederate states that were attacked. <br /><br />It is Gelanor who is provided with the task of cultivating spies for the Trojans especially prostitutes, counter-intelligence measures. Gelanor. He is also the engineer for unconventional warfare. Gelanor states that spies have to inconspicuous and not be vain since vanity in wanting to be reconize can give away the spy. Gelanor loyalty to Helen is commendable given they feel that it is they who are in a civil war since they are both Greeks. Even though Helen lost her family to death b/c of her actions, she so blinded by love that she would do it over again. Gelanor is correct in stating tradition and nobility can hinder progress if it does not take into account new information that has the potential to make things better. He thinks that Hector is too noble about the ways of war and thinks that is not the way to win wars.<br /><br />Not knowing the facts, Paris made a liar out of Priam when he rebuffed the Spartan that they did not have Helen. While Priam eventually warms to Helen, Hecuba knowing the prophesy remains distant toward Helen. While Hecuba prefers tradition as a way to order the world, Helen is individual passion incarnate. Like a great politician who uses sweeping rhetoric to convince others to his point of view, Priam weaves a story out of half-truths in order for his family to accept Helen of Troy. Priam is correct in stating that Troy has to defend all of Troy whereas the Greeks only brought their men to Troy. Once Troilus died and the siege of Troy lasted 1 year, every Trojan even Paris abandoned her except the practical Hector and Andromache. <br /><br />Paris hates prophesies and prefers to live life in the here and now because his is one of bad omens. By favoring Aphrodite to Athena, Paris unwittingly doomed Troy since she is the Troy's protector and would ensure the defeat of Troy from the inside-out via the Trojan horse. Paris finally tells his family about how the gods decreed war by having Paris decide on who is fairest among the goddesses. Whereas Athena and Hera promised Paris victories and material wealth, Aphrodite promised Helen. From the very beginning, deception plagued Paris and Helen's union from Helen leaving Menelaus for Paris, to Paris leaving Oenone for Helen to Paris not telling Helen that she was the prize for choosing Aphrodite thus stacking the cards against her. It seems that the Greek gods are a fickle bunch and they were only worshiped out of fear of incurring their wrath. It seems all it takes is one event in order for something to spin out of control of its originator into fates hands.<br /><br />At least Trojans are not hypocrites about children they sire out-of-wedlock unlike Greek kings who leave their bastards to rot and be sold as slave as Menelaus would do to his child by a slave.<br /><br />Paris wants to build Helen her own palace so they can get lost in the moment in each others embrace and to protect them from the evils both external and internal who want to break their love apart. I guess after 9 years cohabiting in a loveless marriage, when Helen finally found a person who she desired and loved a person cling to it for dear life and savors it day-to-day. In a perfect world in which lovers can live in a warm cocoon, Helen and Paris could live together undisturbed but unfortunately for them the real world brings disastrous consequences to their forbidden love. Helen loves how war created a man out of Paris. I am sure that as it becomes increasingly clear that their love is doomed, it made the love affair that more special. Helen loves that Paris does things in the moment and is filled with joy when he does do it. Helen loves Paris' empathy towards other people. Even though Helen loves Paris, the price for that love is too high from giving Agamemnon just cause to attack Troy, to the death of Leda her sons, and Helen's niece by Iphigenia to satisfy Agamemnon's lust for war, to Paris' brother Troilus death. Paris increased risk of death heightened Helen's desire for him. With Agamemnon gone due to his warmongering and as he sacrificed his only child Iphigenia due to his lust for war, Clytemnestra took on a new lover.<br /><br />Paris finally does the right thing and challenges Menelaus for the right to be with Helen. Before he was defeated by Menelaus, Paris fled; thus Hector was pissed off that Paris did not fight. Although Paris started to learn the real skills of a warrior at Hector's side, he and Helen became the bane of Troy's existence. As the war dragged on for a year even the boys who lusted for war grew weary of the love match that brought war into their midst so much so that both Trojan and Greek prayed that Paris would die in the dual with Menelaus.<br /><br />Hector is the honorable, traditional, steeped in duty to protect Troy from its enemies thus he does not blame Helen and Paris for the Trojan war. He relies on the bravery of the individual and is correct that it is in battle that soldiers become soldiers. Hector alone among the Trojan's seem to know the high cost of war and thus alone wants to defend his home not to start needless wars. Unlike the Greeks who tried to control their fates, the Trojan's accepted theirs. Hector and Andromache were finally blessed with a son. I like Hector because he knows that it his duty to defend Troy and knows as the leader of the army he has to fight to boost morale of his troops but at the same time he knows that war is costly so he would rather avoid it if he could. Hector is really the ideal commander-in-chief because he is ready to risk his life for his country he loves but does so knowing the high cost of war. Unlike Hector, Achilles has no honor. Achilles fights for his own glory not for any cause of for a Greek alliance. For Helen, she rightfully fears Odysseus as the great strategist that he is. According to Hector, Helen is a survivor so she needs to take Andromache under her protection when Troy falls. <br /><br />Because the Trojan alliance finally came to Troy's defense, they had enough men for a frontal assault against the Greeks. But because the Greeks were losing due to a sudden onslaught reeked by the Trojan alliance, Patroclus decided to put on Achilles armor to rally the Greek troops and thus was killed by Hector. Achilles was so pissed off at the death of Patroclus that his mere presence in the battle field caused the Trojan alliance to flight. During the Trojan flight, Achilles killed Hector and dragged his corpse behind his chariot. With the death of Hector, Andromache became a shade of her former self and stayed away from Helen until Paris' death.<br /><br />George made Achilles into self-serving Randian acolyte who only cared for the glory of war including old for the ransom of Hector body. He believes himself to be immortal with the gods favoring him because of his many victories. Amazon warriors came to reinforce the Trojan alliance with their Queen. Penthsesilia thinks that the Trojan alliance gave Achilles power that he himself did not possess by fleeing the battle field at the sight of him. The Amazon's were the original women's lib who did not need men in their midst to survive. The Queen did not care whether or not she died as long as Achilles died. Perhaps beside his supernatural skill as a warrior, Achilles is also a great tactician in scouting the Amazon Queen Penthesileia fought before slaying her. Perhaps due to a prophecy that linked the Queens death with his own demise, Achilles was sad and respectful towards the death of Pennthesileia. With Penthesilieia and Memnon's death hope was extinguished. <br /><br />Paris think fate is fate and nothing can change it. Is it truly the gods and fate who controls our destiny or is it our self-actualization of our proposed destiny that does it? When Helen prays to Zeus to save Paris, he responds that he cannot change men's fate as an enlightened Deist would have us believe.<br /><br />Paris finally killed Achilles with a neck wound not the famous Achilles heel. By doing this and Atenor as possible Helen's real father, George calls into question the Greek mythology of demi-divinity of her heroes. Paris wants to be recognized for his contribution to the Trojan cause by killing of Achilles by his parents even though it was his parents who abandoned him to death at his young age. Is Hecuba's account accurate that a man must prove himself though the gods choose the time and place for them to prove themselves?<br /><br />Helen is the only one who believed in Paris as a man. I think this is the most important quality in a wife; the belief in her husband potential. It is this potential realized that Helen loved as Paris became a great warrior and killed the mighty Achilles. After his death, Paris became the great Trojan warrior catapulting him to the crowned prince position. His skills on the battle field also improved over time. But the time came when Paris was killed by the arrow of Hercules that was head poisoned and caused septicemia in Paris. While Helen and his parents genuinely grieves for Paris' loss, the rest of Troy disdain him for bringing war to Troy. I like how George paints Helen as genuinely distressed at Paris' demise.<br /><br />After Paris' death, Priam implored Helen to be the bride of Deiphobus as his price for defending Troy. Helen has become a prisoner of Troy that must marry Deiphobus to keep her favor among the royals. Even though Helen is a Greek, she hopes the Trojans win since a sacked city means its utter destruction, wholesale killing of men, children, and the elderly while the its women are raped then sold into slavery. I think it is hilarious how the lustful Deiphobus succumbs to Gelanor's treachery with his plants to make him permanently impotent; thus making a mockery of his marriage to Helen.<br /><br />Because the Greeks morale was really low due to the biological warfare that Gelanor employed against them, they had to use treachery of using the Trojan horse as a ploy to win the war. Helen states that a noble nature can lend itself to be a handicap in matters of war and statecraft? Despite the fishiness of the Trojan horse, the Trojans wanted to believe that they were victorious because every Trojan was sick of war and being bottled up in the city of Troy. The brazen surprise attacks by the Greeks is cowardly because they waited until their enemies were incapable of defending themselves due being drunk. The rules of war used to be how the victors go the spoils including raping and auctioning them off to slavery and piliging of its treasure. <br /><br />Aphrodite adopted Helen because she is Aphrodite's essence and being; thus protecting her life. Menelaus sought to torture Helen by keeping her alive while Troy fell around her. She is Menelaus prize for winning the war. With the fall of Troy, Helen states gone is the innocence and excitement of youth only to be replaced by cunning, strategy, perseverance: the gifts of age and disillusionment. Neoptolemus, son of Achilles, broke into Priam's palace killed Priam and his sons and rounded all their princesses to be sold into auction. While Neoptolemus kept Andromache for himself since she was the wife of Troy's greatest warrior, Hector, and he gave Cassandra to Agamemnon, he demanded that Polyxena be sacrificed to death as symbol of the war ending just as Agamemnon's daughter was need to die in order to begin the war. Neoptolemus killed the Hector's son.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5541180031230503245" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('9UVf3OJmrYavf0T8APaUPI0mTl27p7wZd9sYQDZObvMBv1No3ZP8uTRZ2h1PYRKI1seg6RDskYgCxebPAgWltg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('p4yap6iEU7HmkWM8bOg1wsarmg73uPRH7jlpetU76J1TdpYTl3ECjn23/d0jf7N2nUp0ulzz2dabJ5f14XAj2A==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/6599.Margaret_George">About Margaret George</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/6599.Margaret_George"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1273256433p3/6599.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/6599.Margaret_George">Margaret George
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15198071153971829954">Margaret George is a rolling stone who has lived in many places, beginning her traveling at the age of four when her father joined the U.S. diplomatic service and was posted to a consulate in Taiwan. The family traveled on a freighter named after Ulysses' son Telemachus that took thirty days to reach Taiwan, where they spent two years. Following that they lived in Tel Aviv (right after the 1948 wa</span>
  <span id="freeText15198071153971829954" style="display:none">Margaret George is a rolling stone who has lived in many places, beginning her traveling at the age of four when her father joined the U.S. diplomatic service and was posted to a consulate in Taiwan. The family traveled on a freighter named after Ulysses' son Telemachus that took thirty days to reach Taiwan, where they spent two years. Following that they lived in Tel Aviv (right after the 1948 war, when it was relatively quiet), Bonn and Berlin (during the spy-and-Cold-War days) before returning--at the height of Elvis-mania--to Washington DC, where Margaret went to high school. Margaret's first piece of published writing, at the age of thirteen, was a letter to TIME Magazine defending Elvis against his detractors. (Margaret has since been to Graceland.)<br /><br />But it was earlier in Israel that Margaret, an avid reader, began writing novels to amuse herself when she ran out of books to read. Interestingly, the subject of these was not what lay around her in the Middle East, but the American west, which she had never set foot in. (Now that she lives in the American Midwest she writes about the Middle East!) Clearly writing in her case followed Emily Dickinson's observation "There is no frigate like a book" and she used it to go to faraway places. Now she has added another dimension to that travel by specializing in visiting times remote from herself.<br /><br />Neither of these horse sagas got published, but the ten-year-old author received an encouraging note from an editor at Grosset &amp; Dunlap, telling her she had a budding talent but should work on her spelling.<br /><br />It was also in Israel that Margaret started keeping land tortoises as pets, an interest which she still follows today. She had a great affinity for animals and nature and that led her to a double major at Tufts University in English literature and biology. Following that she received an MA in ecology from Stanford University--one of the earliest departments to offer such a concentration. Today she is active in environmental and animal conservation groups.<br /><br />Combining her interests led her to a position as a science writer at the National Cancer Institute (National Institutes of Health) in Bethesda, Maryland for four years.<br /><br />Her marriage at the end of that time meant moving, first to St. Louis, then to Uppsala, Sweden, and then to Madison, Wisconsin, where she and her husband Paul have lived for more than twenty years now. They have one grown daughter who lives in California and is in graduate school.<br /><br />Through all this Margaret continued to write, albeit slowly and always on only one project at a time. She wrote what she refers to as her 'Ayn Rand/adventure novel' in college and her 'Sex and the City' novel in Washington DC. It was in St. Louis that she suddenly got the idea of writing a 'psycho-biography' of Henry VIII. She had never seen such a thing done but became convinced the king was a victim of bad PR and she should rescue his good name. Her background in science meant that only after thoroughly researching the literature and scholarship on Henry VIII would she embark on the novel itself. She sought the guidance of a Tudor historian at Washington University for a reading list, and proceeded from there.<br /><br />It was actually fourteen years between her initial idea and the publication of The Autobiography of Henry VIII. The book made an impression for several reasons: first, because no one had ever written a novel sympathetic to the king before; second, because it covered his entire life from before birth until after his death, making it almost a thousand pages long, and third, because it was so fact-filled.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15198071153971829954" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
